class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/25/us/politics/trump-impeachment-inquiry.html?action=click&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Article" title=""><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">impeachment inquiry</em></a><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0"> with Wednesday’s live updates.</em></p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">WASHINGTON — Speaker Nancy Pelosi announced Tuesday that the House would launch a formal <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/25/us/politics/ukraine-transcript-trump.html?module=inline" title="">impeachment inquiry</a> in response to the dispute over <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/25/us/politics/ukraine-transcript-trump.html?module=inline" title="">Mr. Trump’s</a> efforts to pressure Ukraine to investigate his potential 2020 rival, former Vice President Joseph R. Biden Jr. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The rising furor has heightened interest in how the impeachment process works. Here’s what you need to know:</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-69daf7a">What is impeachment?</h2><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Constitution permits Congress to remove presidents before their term is up if enough lawmakers vote to say that they committed “treason, bribery, or other high crimes and misdemeanors.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Only two presidents have been <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/25/us/politics/ukraine-transcript-trump.html?module=inline" title="">impeached</a> — Andrew Johnson in 1868 and Bill Clinton in 1998 — and both were ultimately acquitted and completed their terms in office. Richard M. Nixon resigned in 1974 to avoid being impeached.</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-651ca7ec">What is a “high crime”?</h2><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The term “high crimes and misdemeanors” came out of the British common law tradition: it was the sort of offense that Parliament cited in removing crown officials for centuries. Essentially, it means an abuse of power by a high-level public official. This does not necessarily have to be a violation of an ordinary criminal statute. </p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In 1788, as supporters of the Constitution were urging states to ratify the document, Alexander Hamilton <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://avalon.law.yale.edu/18th_century/fed65.asp" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">described</a> impeachable crimes in one of the Federalist Papers as “those offenses which proceed from the misconduct of public men, or, in other words, from the abuse or violation of some public trust. They are of a nature which may with peculiar propriety be denominated POLITICAL, as they relate chiefly to injuries done immediately to the society itself.”</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-a9ccfa3">What is the process?</h2><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In both the Nixon and the Clinton cases, the House Judiciary Committee first held an investigation and recommended articles of impeachment to the full House. In theory, however, the House of Representatives could instead set up a special panel to handle the proceedings — or just hold a floor vote on such articles without any committee vetting them.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">When the full House votes on articles of impeachment, if at least one gets a majority vote, the president is impeached — which is essentially the equivalent of being indicted.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><section class="interactive-content interactive-size-scoop css-16boluj" data-id="100000006734806" id="impeachment-process"><header class="css-cl76n0 interactive-header" id="interactive-header"><h2 class="css-1su19vv interactive-headline" id="interactive-headline">How the Impeachment Process Could Play Out</h2></header><div class="css-17ih8de interactive-body" data-sourceid="100000006734806">

<p class="g-pstyle0">strongest cases to the Judiciary Committee.</p>
</div>

<p class="g-pstyle0">determined to be </p>

<script type="text/javascript">
	(function (containerId, opts) {
		if (!('querySelector' in document)) return;
		var container = document.getElementById(containerId);
		var nameSpace = opts.namespace || '';
		var onResize = throttle(update, 200);
		var waiting = !!window.IntersectionObserver;
		var observer;
		update();

		document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', update);
		window.addEventListener('resize', onResize);

		// NYT Scoop-specific code
		if (opts.setup) {
			opts.setup(container).on('cleanup', cleanup);
		}

		function cleanup() {
			document.removeEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', update);
			window.removeEventListener('resize', onResize);
			if (observer) observer.disconnect();
		}

		function update() {
			var artboards = selectChildren('.' + nameSpace + 'artboard[data-min-width]', container),
					width = Math.round(container.getBoundingClientRect().width);

			// Set artboard visibility based on container width
			artboards.forEach(function(el) {
				var minwidth = el.getAttribute('data-min-width'),
						maxwidth = el.getAttribute('data-max-width');
				if (+minwidth <= width && (+maxwidth >= width || maxwidth === null)) {
					if (!waiting) {
						selectChildren('.' + nameSpace + 'aiImg', el).forEach(updateImgSrc);
					}
					el.style.display = 'block';
				} else {
					el.style.display = 'none';
				}
			});

			// Initialize lazy loading on first call
			if (waiting && !observer) {
				if (elementInView(container)) {
					waiting = false;
					update();
				} else {
					observer = new IntersectionObserver(onIntersectionChange, {});
					observer.observe(container);
				}
			}
		}

		function elementInView(el) {
			var bounds = el.getBoundingClientRect();
			return bounds.top < window.innerHeight && bounds.bottom > 0;
		}

		// Replace blank placeholder image with actual image
		function updateImgSrc(img) {
			var src = img.getAttribute('data-src');
			if (src && img.getAttribute('src') != src) {
				img.setAttribute('src', src);
			}
		}

		function onIntersectionChange(entries) {
			if (entries.length && entries[0].isIntersecting) {
				waiting = false;
				observer.disconnect();
				observer = null;
				update();
			}
		}

		function selectChildren(selector, parent) {
			return parent ? Array.prototype.slice.call(parent.querySelectorAll(selector)) : [];
		}

		// based on underscore.js
		function throttle(func, wait) {
			var timeout = null, previous = 0;
			function run() {
					previous = Date.now();
					timeout = null;
					func();
			}
			return function() {
				var remaining = wait - (Date.now() - previous);
				if (remaining <= 0 || remaining > wait) {
					clearTimeout(timeout);
					run();
				} else if (!timeout) {
					timeout = setTimeout(run, remaining);
				}
			};
		}
	})("g-impeachment-box", {namespace: "g-", setup: window.setupInteractive || window.getComponent});
</script>

<!-- Pipeline: 2019-09-24-impeachment-process | September 25, 2019, 12:27PM | acc7f7dc8b447b0607691436336b68d96bf939a5 -->
</div><footer class="css-irejme interactive-footer" id="interactive-footer"><p class="css-1g8gl4z interactive-credit" data-testid="credit" id="interactive-credit">By The New York Times</p></footer></section><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Next, the proceedings move to the Senate, which is to hold a trial overseen by the chief justice of the United States.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">A team of lawmakers from the House, known as managers, play the role of prosecutors. The president has defense lawyers, and the Senate serves as the jury.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">If at least two-thirds of the senators find the president guilty, he is removed, and the vice president takes over as president. There is no appeal.</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-6b6d8626">How does a House impeachment inquiry start?</h2></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/24/us/politics/24dc-explainer3/merlin_160673460_822b79c8-4b6a-4749-b667-ab95816eaff2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><img alt="Representative Jerrold Nadler, the chairman of the Judiciary Committee, has claimed that the panel is already engaged in an impeachment investigation." class="css-1m50asq" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/24/us/politics/24dc-explainer3/merlin_160673460_822b79c8-4b6a-4749-b667-ab95816eaff2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" itemprop="url" sizes="((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 60vw, 100vw" src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/24/us/politics/24dc-explainer3/merlin_160673460_822b79c8-4b6a-4749-b667-ab95816eaff2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" srcset="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/24/us/politics/24dc-explainer3/merlin_160673460_822b79c8-4b6a-4749-b667-ab95816eaff2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/24/us/politics/24dc-explainer3/merlin_160673460_822b79c8-4b6a-4749-b667-ab95816eaff2-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/24/us/politics/24dc-explainer3/merlin_160673460_822b79c8-4b6a-4749-b667-ab95816eaff2-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w"/></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">Representative Jerrold Nadler, the chairman of the Judiciary Committee, has claimed that the panel is already engaged in an impeachment investigation.</span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>Erin Schaff/The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/13/us/politics/trump-impeachment-definition.html?module=inline" title="">This has been a subject of dispute</a>. During the Nixon and Clinton impeachment efforts, the full House voted for resolutions directing the House Judiciary Committee to open the inquiries. But it is not clear whether that step is strictly necessary, because impeachment proceedings against other officials, like a former federal judge in 1989, began at the committee level. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The House Judiciary Committee, led by Representative Jerrold Nadler, Democrat of New York, has <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/07/26/us/politics/donald-trump-impeachment.html?module=inline" title="">claimed</a> — including in court filings — that the panel is already engaged in an impeachment investigation. Mr. Trump’s Justice Department has <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/13/us/politics/trump-impeachment-definition.html?module=inline" title="">argued</a> that since there has been no House resolution, the committee is just engaged in a routine oversight proceeding.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Pelosi did not say in her announcement that she intended to bring any resolution to the floor.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Whether or not it is necessary, it has not been clear whether a resolution to formally start an impeachment inquiry would pass a House vote, although the number of Democrats who support one has recently been surging. As of late Tuesday, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2019/05/31/us/politics/trump-impeachment-congress-list.html?module=inline" title="">The New York Times counted</a> 203 members who said they favored impeachment proceedings, 88 who said they opposed them or were undecided, and 144 who had not responded to the question.</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-52fc9689">What are the rules for a Senate trial? </h2><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">There are no set rules. Rather, the Senate passes a resolution first laying out trial procedures.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“When the Senate decided what the rules were going to be for our trial, they really made them up as they went along,” <!-- -->Gregory B. Craig<!-- -->, who helped defend Mr. Clinton in his impeachment proceeding and later served as White House counsel to President Barack Obama, told The Times in 2017.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">For example, Mr. Craig said, the initial rules in that case gave Republican managers four days to make a case for conviction, followed by four days for the president’s legal team to defend him. These were essentially opening statements. The Senate then decided whether to hear witnesses, and if so, whether it would be live or on videotape. Eventually, the Senate permitted each side to depose several witnesses by videotape.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The rules adopted by the Senate in the Clinton trial — including ones limiting the number of witnesses and the length of depositions — made it harder to prove a case compared with trials in federal court, said former Representative Bob Barr, Republican of Georgia who served as a House manager during the trial and is also a former United States attorney.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“Impeachment is a creature unto itself,” Mr. Barr said. “The jury in a criminal case doesn’t set the rules for a case and can’t decide what evidence they want to see and what they won’t.”</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-4eee7855">What are the standards for impeachment and removal?<!-- --> </h2><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Constitution does not specify many, making impeachment and removal as much a question of political will as of legal analysis. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">For example, the Constitution does not detail how lawmakers may choose to interpret what does or does not constitute impeachable “treason, bribery, or other high crimes and misdemeanors.” Similarly, there is no established standard of proof that must be met.</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-2065efa4">Is the Senate obligated to hold a trial?</h2><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Constitution clearly envisions that if the House impeaches a federal official, the next step is for the Senate to hold a trial. But there is no obvious enforcement mechanism if Senator Mitch McConnell, Republican of Kentucky and the majority leader, were to simply refuse to convene one — just as he refused to permit a confirmation hearing and vote on Mr. Obama’s nominee, Judge Merrick Garland, to fill a Supreme Court vacancy in 2016.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Still Walter Dellinger, a Duke University law professor and a former acting solicitor general in the Clinton administration, said it is unclear whether it would be Mr. McConnell or Chief Justice John G. Roberts Jr. who wields the authority to convene the Senate for the purpose of considering House-passed articles of impeachment. </p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Either way, though, he noted that the Republican majority in the Senate could vote to immediately dismiss the case without any consideration of the evidence if it wanted.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">To date, Senate Republicans have given no indication that they would break with Mr. Trump, especially in numbers sufficient to remove him from office. In their internal debate about what to do, some Democrats have argued that this political reality means that they should instead focus on trying to beat him in the 2020 election, on the theory that an acquittal in the Senate might backfire by strengthening him politically. Others have argued that impeaching him is a moral necessity to deter future presidents from acting like Mr. Trump, even if Senate Republicans are likely to keep him in office.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In that same Federalist Paper written in 1788, Mr. Hamilton wrote that the inherently political nature of impeachment proceedings would be sure to polarize the country.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Their prosecution, he wrote, “will seldom fail to agitate the passions of the whole community, and to divide it into parties more or less friendly or inimical to the accused. In many cases it will connect itself with the pre-existing factions, and will enlist all their animosities, partialities, influence and interest on one side or on the other; and in such cases there will always be the greatest danger that the decision will be regulated more by the comparative strength of parties, than by the real demonstrations of innocence or guilt.”</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-g92qtk epkadsg3"><div class="css-1owp1gq epkadsg0">Related Coverage</div><div class="css-15g2oxy epkadsg2"><div class="css-2b3w4o e16ij5yr6"><a     window.Raven.captureException(err.err, err.data || {});
  } // else { silently ignore? }

}); // Pass collected Tags to Raven

window.nyt_errors.tags.forEach(function (tag) {
  window.Raven.setTagsContext(tag);
});
    };
    includeRaven.appendChild(script);
  });
}
</script>
</div>
</body>
</html>
